      <br><a id="affa"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">a</span></sup></a><span class="italic">Key Laboratory of Colloid and Interface Chemistry Ministry of Education, School of Chemistry and Chemical Engineering, Shandong University, Jinan 250100, P. R. China</span>
      <br><a id="affb"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">b</span></sup></a><span class="italic">School of Physical and Mathematical Sciences, Nanyang Technological University, Singapore 637371, Singapore. E-mail: <a href="mailto:jun.pan@ntu.edu.sg">jun.pan@ntu.edu.sg</a></span>
    </p><div id="art-admin"><span class="italic bold">Received 
      11th December 2023
    </span><span class="bold italic">, Accepted 7th March 2024</span></div><p class="bold italic">First published on 5th April 2024</p><hr><div class="abstract"><h2>Abstract</h2><p>The thermodynamic instability of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>-intercalated compounds is an important factor limiting the application of graphite anodes in sodium-ion batteries. Although solvent co-intercalation is recognized as a simple and effective strategy, the challenge lies in the lack of durable electrolytes. Herein, we successfully apply low-concentration imidazole-based electrolytes to graphite anodes for sodium-ion batteries. Specifically, low concentrations ensure high ionic conductivity while saving on costs. Methylimidazole molecules can be co-intercalated with Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>, and a small amount of unreleased solvated Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> serves the dual purpose of providing support to the graphite layer and preventing peeling off. The interphase formed in imidazole is more uniform and dense compared with that in ether electrolytes, which reduces side reactions and the risk of internal short circuits. The obtained battery demonstrates a long cycle life of 1800 cycles with a capacity retention of 84.6%. This success extends to other imidazole-based solvents such as 1-propylimidazole and 1-butylimidazole.</p></div><hr>
    
      <h2 id="sect232"><span class="a_heading">Introduction</span></h2>
      <span>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are emerging as a promising alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in power and energy storage applications.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1–3</span></sup></a> The growing interest in SIBs stems from their similar production processes to LIBs, as well as their lower production costs.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit4"><sup><span class="sup_ref">4–6</span></sup></a> Graphite, renowned for its commendable conductivity and cost-effectiveness, stands out as a well-established commercial anode material for LIBs. However, its sodium storage capacity in conventional ester electrolytes is nearly negligible. This limitation is attributed to the thermodynamic instability of Na–graphite intercalation compounds (GICs).<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit7"><sup><span class="sup_ref">7,8</span></sup></a></span>
      <p class="otherpara">To mitigate the adverse interaction between Na and graphite, several strategies have been proposed, such as expanding the graphite interlayer and achieving the co-intercalation of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> and solvents. While expanded graphite demonstrates favorable sodium storage behavior, its synthesis process is complex, resulting in poor product consistency and environmental concerns.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit9"><sup><span class="sup_ref">9,10</span></sup></a> Another simple and effective method involves creating solvated sodium ion structures capable of intercalating between graphite layers. Currently, ether solvents are recognized molecules capable of intercalation in a solvated form.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit11"><sup><span class="sup_ref">11–13</span></sup></a> Jache <span class="italic">et al.</span> presented the co-intercalation phenomena of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> and ether-based solvents to overcome the mismatch of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> and graphite lattice distance.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit14"><sup><span class="sup_ref">14</span></sup></a> The co-intercalation is attributed to the strong solvation effect between sodium ions and ether molecules,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit15"><sup><span class="sup_ref">15</span></sup></a> as well as the nearly no solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film formed by graphite in ether electrolytes.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit16"><sup><span class="sup_ref">16</span></sup></a> However, challenges, including material peeling and the risk of internal short circuits (due to an inability to charge to the set voltage), are encountered in the sodium storage of graphite in ether-based electrolytes.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit17"><sup><span class="sup_ref">17</span></sup></a> Therefore, there is an urgent need to develop an economical and stable electrolyte system to achieve efficient sodium storage in graphite materials.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">Herein, a new low-concentration imidazole-based electrolyte is screened based on donor number (DN), dielectric constant, and ionic conductivity, which proves the cycle stability of graphite anodes in sodium-ion batteries. Firstly, the use of a low-concentration salt ensures high ionic conductivity while reducing the electrolyte cost. Secondly, the solvated Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ions, initially intercalated but partially unreleased, serve a dual purpose. They provide structural support to the graphite layer, facilitating the intercalation/deintercalation of solvated sodium ions in subsequent cycles, thereby improving rate performance. Additionally, these unreleased solvated-Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ions interact with 1-methylimidazole (Melm) molecules, hindering the peeling of the graphite layer and contributing to enhanced cycle stability. Thirdly, in comparison to ether electrolytes, the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer formed in Melm exhibits greater uniformity, diminishing side reactions and mitigating the risk of internal short circuits during cycling. Based on 0.2 M electrolyte, the obtained half-cell (C//Na) achieves a long life of over 1800 cycles (∼84.6% capacity retention) and the full cell (NaTi<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>3</sub></small>//C) shows a long cycle life of over 2800 cycles. Furthermore, encouraging results are also obtained when extended to 1-propylimidazole (Prlm) and 1-butyimidazole (Bulm) solvents. These findings offer valuable insights for the selection of new electrolytes in sodium-ion battery applications.</p>
    
    
      <h2 id="sect255"><span class="a_heading">Results and discussion</span></h2>
      
        <h3 id="sect257"><span class="b_heading">Electrolyte properties and electrochemical performance</span></h3>
        <span>To achieve co-intercalation of solvent and Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>, the solvent needs to be strongly coordinated with Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit18"><sup><span class="sup_ref">18</span></sup></a> Two important parameters measuring solvation ability are DN and dielectric constant. Larger values indicate stronger solvation.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit19"><sup><span class="sup_ref">19</span></sup></a> Melm has a higher DN and higher dielectric constant, which can meet the requirements (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1a</a>). In addition, Melm exhibits lower viscosity and a more affordable price than other solvents (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1b</a>). The ionic conductivity exceeds that of traditional ether solvents at the same salt concentration (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1c</a>). After comprehensive consideration, Melm emerges as the most suitable choice. Salt concentration crucially influences electrolyte cost, making it pivotal to achieve Melm application at low concentrations. Hence, three common sodium salts (NaPF<small><sub>6</sub></small>, NaCF<small><sub>3</sub></small>SO<small><sub>3</sub></small>, and NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small>) were utilized to prepare the electrolytes (Table S1<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>) to identify the optimal electrolyte. The Raman spectrum revealed an abundance of free anions in 0.1 M NaCF<small><sub>3</sub></small>SO<small><sub>3</sub></small> in Melm (0.1-Melm) and 0.2 M NaCF<small><sub>3</sub></small>SO<small><sub>3</sub></small> in Melm (0.2-Melm) electrolytes (Fig. S1<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>) compared to other concentrations, suggesting sodium ions were surrounded by solvent molecules.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit20"><sup><span class="sup_ref">20</span></sup></a> This environment facilitates sodium ion intercalation into the graphite layer in a solvated form (Fig. S2<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). Although the solvation structure in 0.2-Melm resembles that in 0.1-Melm, the ionic conductivity of 0.2-Melm (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1d</a>) reaches 4.45 mS cm<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, twice that of 0.1-Melm (2.48 mS cm<small><sup>−1</sup></small>). Furthermore, 0.2-Melm exhibits higher capacity and superior cycle stability compared to 0.2 M NaPF<small><sub>6</sub></small> in Melm and 0.2 M NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small> in Melm (Fig. S3 and S4<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). Therefore, 0.2-Melm was chosen for subsequent comparative experiments and characterization.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig1"><a href="/image/article/2024/SC/d3sc06640a/d3sc06640a-f1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3sc06640a-f1.tif" src="/image/article/2024/SC/d3sc06640a/d3sc06640a-f1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 1 </b> <span id="fig1"><span class="graphic_title">(a) DN <span class="italic">versus</span> dielectric constant plot of different solvents. (b) Viscosity <span class="italic">versus</span> price plot of different solvents. (c) Ionic conductivity of 1 M NaSO<small><sub>3</sub></small>CF<small><sub>3</sub></small> in different solvents. (d) Ionic conductivity of NaCF<small><sub>3</sub></small>SO<small><sub>3</sub></small> in Melm with different salt concentrations. (e) CV curves of MCMB anodes in 0.2-Melm and 0.2-DGM. (f) Galvanostatic voltage profiles of MCMB anodes in 0.2-Melm and 0.2-DGM at 0.1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. (g) Initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) comparison diagram. (h) Long-term cycling performances of MCMB electrodes in 0.2-Melm and 0.2-DGM.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">Given that ethers are recognized solvents for solvation intercalation, diglyme (DGM) was selected as the reference for electrochemical performance (Fig. S5<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). A comparative analysis reveals that 0.2-Melm exhibits lower viscosity, higher ionic conductivity, and superior solvation capability compared to 0.2-DGM (Fig. S6–S8<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). Next, mesophase carbon microbeads (MCMB) were employed as the anode to assess electrochemical performance using 0.2-Melm and 0.2-DGM electrolytes (Fig. S9<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). The redox peaks in the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves represent the formation of different graphite interlayer compounds. Notably, the polarization voltage in 0.2-Melm is smaller than that in 0.2-DGM, indicating faster reaction kinetics in 0.2-Melm (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1e</a>).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit21"><sup><span class="sup_ref">21</span></sup></a> In contrast to the ether electrolyte, MCMB in 0.2-Melm has an additional pair of redox peaks at 0.05/0.1 V, gradually disappearing with increasing current (Fig. S10<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). This suggests an additional Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> intercalation reaction at low voltages.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit22"><sup><span class="sup_ref">22</span></sup></a> The initial coulombic efficiency of MCMB in 0.2-Melm is 87.6% (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1f</a>), surpassing that reported in several other articles (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1g</a>).<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit15"><sup><span class="sup_ref">15,23–27</span></sup></a> Moreover, MCMB in 0.2-Melm presents superior rate performance (Fig. S11<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>), demonstrating a capacity of 128.8 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 0.5 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, 123.6.5 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, and 117.1 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 2 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. Impressively, the MCMB anode in 0.2-Melm sustains a long-term cycle performance over 1800 cycles at a current density of 1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, maintaining a capacity of 98.8 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> with a capacity retention of 84.6%. However, the cycle life of MCMB in 0.2-DGM was limited to only 128 cycles and failed due to an internal short circuit (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1h</a>). Thus, an in-depth exploration is needed to identify the reasons for the performance differences mentioned above.</p>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect316"><span class="b_heading">Electrochemical reaction mechanism and interface characterization</span></h3>
        <span>To gain insight into the sodium storage mechanism of MCMB in 0.2-Melm electrolyte, <span class="italic">in situ</span> X-ray diffraction (XRD) measurements were conducted. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2a</a> shows that the entire intercalation and extraction process is a staging behavior, similar to the intercalation of solvated sodium ions in ether.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit25"><sup><span class="sup_ref">25,27</span></sup></a> The peak at 26° in the initial state corresponds to the (002) plane of MCMB.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit28"><sup><span class="sup_ref">28</span></sup></a> During the discharge process, (002) slowly shifts to 25°, and two new peaks (at ∼20° and 30°) gradually appear, representing different stages of intercalation reactions. As the discharging occurs, the ongoing displacement suggests that the insertion of solvated Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> at this stage alters the interlayer spacing.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit29"><sup><span class="sup_ref">29</span></sup></a> Upon discharge to 0.67 V, MCMB shows new peaks at 12.5° and 18.6°, indicative of a new stage of solvated Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>-intercalated graphite. However, unlike at high potential, there is almost no change in interlayer spacing, which is possibly attributable to different solvation forms at this potential.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit27"><sup><span class="sup_ref">27</span></sup></a> During the charging process, the peaks at ∼26° and ∼30° are not fully recovered, suggesting the presence of residual solvated Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> between the layers.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit25"><sup><span class="sup_ref">25</span></sup></a> To determine whether it is co-embedded in a solvated form, <span class="italic">ex situ</span> Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectra were obtained (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2b</a>). To avoid the influence of Melm adsorption, electrodes after cycling were cleaned multiple times with ethanol. When discharged to 0.01 V, the appearance of the C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">N peak of Melm at 1500 cm<small><sup>−1</sup></small> indicates the embedding of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> in a solvated form.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30</span></sup></a> Charging the electrode to 2 V reveals a weak peak of C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">N, confirming the presence of residual solvated sodium ions between the layers, aligning with the XRD pattern. The above mechanisms played a positive role, as evidenced by the superior rate performance compared to ether (Fig. S11<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>), with no cracks in the MCMB particles after 100 cycles (Fig. S12<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). Therefore, the residual solvated Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> proves beneficial to reaction kinetics and interlayer interactions.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig2"><a href="/image/article/2024/SC/d3sc06640a/d3sc06640a-f2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3sc06640a-f2.tif" src="/image/article/2024/SC/d3sc06640a/d3sc06640a-f2.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 2 </b> <span id="fig2"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Discharge/charge curves and corresponding <span class="italic">in situ</span> XRD patterns of the MCMB anode in 0.2-Melm. (b) FTIR spectra of Melm and the MCMB anode at different states. (c) HRTEM images of MCMB in 0.2-Melm and in 0.2-DGM after discharge. (d) EIS spectra of MCMB anodes in 0.2-Melm and in 0.2-DGM after 3 cycles. (e) Discharge/charge curves of MCMB anodes in 0.2-Melm and 0.2-DGM at 1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> in the 128th cycle. (f and g) SEM images of MCMB anodes in 0.2-Melm or in 0.2-DGM at open circuit voltage (OCV) in the 128th cycle.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">The investigation of interface properties remains crucial in understanding and optimizing electrochemical performance. High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images reveal a more uniform solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI) film in 0.2-Melm compared to 0.2-DGM (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2c</a>). Through X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analysis, it was found that the SEI layer is enriched with sodium nitride inorganic content (401.5 eV) (Fig. S13<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>),<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit31"><sup><span class="sup_ref">31,32</span></sup></a> enhancing the mechanical strength and ionic conductivity of the interphase layer. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) of the cells after 3 cycles was performed and simulated.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit33"><sup><span class="sup_ref">33,34</span></sup></a> Results indicate that solution resistance (<span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>s</sub></small>), interface film resistance (<span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>f</sub></small>), and charge transfer resistance (<span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small>) are all smaller when using Melm compared to the ether electrolyte (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2d</a> and Table S2<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). This behavior can be attributed to the solvent properties, the inorganic-rich characteristics of the SEI film, and the co-solvent intercalation mechanism of Melm. The demise of ether electrolyte batteries is elucidated in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1h</a>. Examining the discharge/charge curves of the MCMB anode over 128 cycles, it is evident that in 0.2-Melm, the curves remain normal, while in 0.2-DGM, the battery fails to charge to the set voltage (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2e</a>). This may be due to the uneven SEI layer in 0.2-DGM, causing oxidized carbon atoms in the charged state to dissolve into the electrolyte, migrate to the sodium metal anode, and undergo redox reactions.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit35"><sup><span class="sup_ref">35</span></sup></a> This is supported by NMR results (Fig. S14 and Table S3<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>), with the C content in 0.2-DGM significantly increasing after 128 cycles compared with 0.2-DGM before cycling, indicating that carbon atoms can be dissolved into 0.2-DGM. Then in simpler terms, an internal short circuit occurs due to internal redox reactions. Furthermore, scanning electron microscope (SEM) images, along with side-view SEM images of MCMB under OCV and after 128 cycles in different electrolytes, were collected (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2f and g</a>). Before cycling, the particles are tightly bound to the current collector, but the difference is obvious after 128 cycles. In 0.2-Melm, electrode materials remain closely bound to the current collector, presenting a smooth and flat surface. Conversely, in 0.2-DGM, cracks become apparent on the electrode surface, and the materials detach from the current collector. This may be caused by uneven stress between the active material and the current collector due to the uneven SEI.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit36"><sup><span class="sup_ref">36</span></sup></a></p>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect360"><span class="b_heading">Electrochemical performances of MCMB//NTP/C full cells</span></h3>
        <span>Limited by the decomposition voltage of the electrolyte (Fig. S15<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>), it is unsuitable for a high voltage cathode, so NaTi<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>3</sub></small> (NTP) was chosen as the cathode.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit37"><sup><span class="sup_ref">37</span></sup></a> The synthesized NTP/C exhibits a size of 5–20 μm and a capacity of 130 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at a current density of 0.1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> (Fig. S16<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3a</a> illustrates the testing of full cells within 1.2–2.3 V, with the N<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">P ratio (capacity ratio of anode to cathode) controlled at ∼1.2. Before assembling the full cells, both the anode and cathode undergo activation for 3 cycles. To gain a clearer understanding of the reaction, the capacity <span class="italic">versus</span> voltage (d<span class="italic">Q</span>/d<span class="italic">V</span>) was integrated.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit38"><sup><span class="sup_ref">38</span></sup></a> The redox peaks at 1.35/1.4 V and 2.0–2.1/2.15 V correspond to the co-intercalation/de-intercalation of solvated Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> in graphite (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3b</a>). In terms of rate performance (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3c</a>), the MCMB//NTP/C cell displays a capacity of 90.9 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 0.1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, 61.5 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 0.5 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, 39.4 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, and 15.4 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 2 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. The capacity can be restored when the current density returns to 0.1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, and all capacities are obtained based on the mass of MCMB materials. With increasing current, the polarization voltage gradually rises (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3d</a>), and the MCMB//NTP/C cell shows a capacity retention of 46.7% after 2800 cycles at 0.5 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3e</a>). The stable cycling performance is related to the formation of a robust SEI film and the co-solvent intercalation mechanism in 0.2 Melm.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig3"><a href="/image/article/2024/SC/d3sc06640a/d3sc06640a-f3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3sc06640a-f3.tif" src="/image/article/2024/SC/d3sc06640a/d3sc06640a-f3.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 3 </b> <span id="fig3"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Schematic diagram of the discharge process. (b) d<span class="italic">Q</span>/d<span class="italic">V</span> curves for the first cycle. (c) Rate performances. (d) Charge/discharge curves at different current densities. (e) Cycling performance at 0.5 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect396"><span class="b_heading">Properties of Melm derivatives as electrolytes</span></h3>
        <span>Motivated by the favorable performance of the 0.2 Melm electrolyte, we explored the potential of imidazole derivatives, specifically Prlm and Bulm, as electrolyte solvents in SIBs due to their similar molecular structures. It is necessary to investigate whether there is a strong binding energy between Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> and solvent molecules. The binding energies and the ESP<small><sub>min</sub></small> of Prlm/Bulm and Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> were calculated by density functional theory (DFT). The binding energies for Prlm and Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> and Bulm and Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> are −1.12 and −1.13 eV, respectively (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4a</a>). The values for the ESP<small><sub>min</sub></small> of Prlm and Bulm are −56.53 and −56.63 kcal mol<small><sup>−1</sup></small> (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4b</a>). The high binding energy and low ESP<small><sub>min</sub></small> indicate that both Prlm and Bulm have strong binding energy with Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit39"><sup><span class="sup_ref">39,40</span></sup></a> Then, the electrochemical performances of MCMB anodes in 0.2-Prlm and 0.2-Bulm were measured. At a current density of 0.1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, the capacity of MCMB anodes in 0.2-Prlm (116 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>) surpassed that in 0.2-Bulm (90 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>), which is potentially attributable to solvation forms and steric effects.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit41"><sup><span class="sup_ref">41</span></sup></a> The ICEs of MCMB anodes in 0.2-Prlm and 0.2-Bulm are 76.3% and 83.3% (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4c and e</a>), which may be related to the different thickness of the SEI layer formed.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit42"><sup><span class="sup_ref">42,43</span></sup></a> The CV curves of 0.2-Prlm and 0.2-Bulm were similar to those of 0.2-Melm (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4d and f</a>), signifying the occurrence of intercalation and deintercalation reactions of solvated sodium ions. The dissimilarity in the first cycle can be attributed to SEI formation, while subsequent cycles exhibit consistent reversibility. Different redox peaks in CV curves represent the reaction of solvated Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> of different stages, that is, the formation of different GICs.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit44"><sup><span class="sup_ref">44</span></sup></a> Melm derivatives (0.2-Melm, 0.2-Prlm, and 0.2-Bulm) exhibited good rate performance with little capacity fading as the current increases (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4g</a>). The long-term cycle performance of MCMB anodes in Melm derivatives as electrolytes remained stable without short circuits after 300 cycles at a current density of 1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4h</a>). Therefore, imidazole-based electrolytes have promising potential for applications in medium-voltage sodium-ion batteries.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig4"><a href="/image/article/2024/SC/d3sc06640a/d3sc06640a-f4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3sc06640a-f4.tif" src="/image/article/2024/SC/d3sc06640a/d3sc06640a-f4.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 4 </b> <span id="fig4"><span class="graphic_title">(a) The binding energy of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> and Prlm/Bulm solvents. (b) Calculated minimum electrostatic potentials (ESP<small><sub>min</sub></small>) of Prlm and Bulm solvents. (c) Galvanostatic voltage profiles of MCMB anode in 0.2-Prlm. (d) CV curves of MCMB anode in 0.2-Prlm. (e) Galvanostatic voltage profiles of MCMB anode in 0.2-Bulm. (f) CV curves of MCMB anode in 0.2-Bulm. (g) Rate performances and (h) long-term cycling performances of MCMB anodes in different imidazole-based electrolytes (Melm, Prlm, and Bulm).</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      
    
    
      <h2 id="sect427"><span class="a_heading">Conclusion</span></h2>
      <span>In summary, 0.2-Melm successfully improves the cycling performances of graphite anodes in SIBs. The Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>-Melm co-intercalation mechanism is substantiated through <span class="italic">in situ</span> XRD patterns and <span class="italic">ex situ</span> FTIR spectra. Notably, the residual solvated Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>, persisting after the initial intercalation, assumes a crucial role in the system. This residual Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> not only expedites the reaction kinetics of subsequent ion insertion but also acts as a protective agent for the graphite layers, mitigating the risk of peeling off. The comparative analysis reveals that in 0.2-Melm, a more uniform SEI layer with superior ionic conductivity is established compared to 0.2-DGM, contributing to the long-term cycling performance. Based on this electrolyte, MBMC//Na batteries exhibit a discharge capacity of 150.5 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and a high ICE of 87.6%. Impressively, the reversible capacity after 1800 cycles remains substantial at 98.8 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, demonstrating an outstanding capacity retention of 84.6%. Furthermore, even an MBMC//NTP/C full cell demonstrates stable cycling for an extended period of 2800 cycles at 0.5 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. In addition, imidazole derivatives Prlm and Bulm exhibit stable long cycles in graphite anodes, surpassing the performance of ether-based electrolytes. Our research introduces a new electrolyte system for sodium-ion batteries.</span>

They are usually only set in response to actions made by you which amount to a request for services, such as setting your privacy preferences, logging in or filling in forms. You can set your browser to block or alert you about these cookies, but some parts of the site will not then work. These cookies do not store any personally identifiable information.</p></div></div><div class="ot-accordion-layout ot-cat-item ot-vs-config" data-optanongroupid="C0002"><button aria-expanded="false" ot-accordion="true" aria-controls="ot-desc-id-C0002" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0002"></button><!-- Accordion header --><div class="ot-acc-hdr"><div class="ot-plus-minus"><span></span><span></span></div><h4 class="ot-cat-header" id="ot-header-id-C0002">Analytical cookies</h4><div class="ot-tgl"><input type="checkbox" name="ot-group-id-C0002" id="ot-group-id-C0002" role="switch" class="category-switch-handler" data-optanongroupid="C0002" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0002"> <label class="ot-switch" for="ot-group-id-C0002"><span class="ot-switch-nob"></span> <span class="ot-label-txt">Analytical cookies</span></label> </div></div><!-- accordion detail --><div class="ot-acc-grpcntr ot-acc-txt"><p class="ot-acc-grpdesc ot-category-desc" id="ot-desc-id-C0002">These cookies allow us to count visits and traffic sources so we can measure and improve the performance of our site. They help us to know which pages are the most and least popular and see how visitors move around the site. All information these cookies collect is aggregated and therefore anonymous.
